<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and analysis of differential homoeolog gene expression influencing selection"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2016 48:10"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2016-09-05"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2016 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Mingfang Zhang, Sally Mackenzie and colleagues report the genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and through comparative analysis suggest that A-subgenome evolution contributes to differences in agricultural subvarieties. They find that differential homoeolog gene expression from the subgenomes helps to shape the selection that distinguishes vegetable- and oil-use Brassica. The Brassica genus encompasses three diploid and three allopolyploid genomes, but a clear understanding of the evolution of agriculturally important traits via polyploidy is lacking. We assembled an allopolyploid Brassica juncea genome by shotgun and single-molecule reads integrated to genomic and genetic maps. We discovered that the A subgenomes of B. juncea and Brassica napus each had independent origins. Results suggested that A subgenomes of B. juncea were of monophyletic origin and evolved into vegetable-use and oil-use subvarieties. Homoeolog expression dominance occurs between subgenomes of allopolyploid B. juncea, in which differentially expressed genes display more selection potential than neutral genes. Homoeolog expression dominance in B. juncea has facilitated selection of glucosinolate and lipid metabolism genes in subvarieties used as vegetables and for oil production. These homoeolog expression dominance relationships among Brassicaceae genomes have contributed to selection response, predicting the directional effects of selection in a polyploid crop genome."/>

        <meta property="og:description" content="Mingfang Zhang, Sally Mackenzie and colleagues report the genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and through comparative analysis suggest that A-subgenome evolution contributes to differences in agricultural subvarieties. They find that differential homoeolog gene expression from the subgenomes helps to shape the selection that distinguishes vegetable- and oil-use Brassica."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>The <i>Brassica</i> genus encompasses three diploid and three allopolyploid genomes, but a clear understanding of the evolution of agriculturally important traits via polyploidy is lacking. We assembled an allopolyploid <i>Brassica juncea</i> genome by shotgun and single-molecule reads integrated to genomic and genetic maps. We discovered that the A subgenomes of <i>B. juncea</i> and <i>Brassica napus</i> each had independent origins. Results suggested that A subgenomes of <i>B. juncea</i> were of monophyletic origin and evolved into vegetable-use and oil-use subvarieties. Homoeolog expression dominance occurs between subgenomes of allopolyploid <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i>, in which differentially expressed genes display more selection potential than neutral genes. Homoeolog expression dominance in <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> has facilitated selection of glucosinolate and lipid metabolism genes in subvarieties used as vegetables and for oil production. These homoeolog expression dominance relationships among Brassicaceae genomes have contributed to selection response, predicting the directional effects of selection in a polyploid crop genome.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The <i>Brassica</i> genus contains a diverse range of oilseed and vegetable crops important for human nutrition<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Warwick, S.I., Francis, A. &amp; Al-Shehbaz, I.A. Brassicaceae: species checklist and database on CDâRom. Plant Syst. Evol. 259, 249â258 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d21220e802">1</a></sup>. Crops of particular agricultural importance include three diploid species, <i>Brassica rapa</i> (AA), <i>Brassica nigra</i> (BB) and <i>Brassica oleracea</i> (CC), and three allopolyploid species, <i>B</i>. <i>napus</i> (AACC), <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> (AABB) and <i>Brassica carinata</i> (BBCC). The evolutionary relationships among these <i>Brassica</i> species are described by what is called the 'triangle of U' model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Nagaharu, U. Genome analysis in Brassica with special reference to the experimental formation of B. napus and peculiar mode of fertilication. Jpn. J. Bot. 7, 389â452 (1935)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d21220e835">2</a></sup>, which proposes how the genomes of the three ancestral <i>Brassica</i> species, <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>B. nigra</i> and <i>Brassica oleracae</i>, combined to give rise to the allopolyploid species of this genus. <i>B. juncea</i> formed by hybridization between the diploid ancestors of <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. nigra</i>, followed by spontaneous chromosome doubling. Subsequent diversifying selection then gave rise to the vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties of <i>B. juncea</i>. These subvarieties include vegetable and oilseed mustard in China, oilseed crops in India, canola crops in Canada and Australia, and condiment crops in Europe and other regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Chen, S. et al. Evidence from genome-wide simple sequence repeat markers for a polyphyletic origin and secondary centers of genetic diversity of Brassica juncea in China and India. J. Hered. 104, 416â427 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d21220e864">3</a></sup>. Cultivation of <i>B. juncea</i> began in China about 6,000 to 7,000 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Institute of Archaeology of Chinese Academy of Science. Xian Banpo country. Special Issue of Archaeology (Archaeology Press, 1963)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d21220e871">4</a></sup>, and flourished in India from 2,300 BC onward<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Prakash, S. &amp; Hinata, K. Taxonomy, cytogenetics and origin of crop Brassicas, a review. Opera Bot. 55, 1â57 (1980)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d21220e876">5</a></sup>.</p><p>The genomes of <i>B</i>. <i>rapa</i>, <i>B</i>. <i>oleracea</i> and their allopolyploid offspring <i>B</i>. <i>napus</i> have been published recently<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d21220e902">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun. 5, 3930 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d21220e905">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d21220e908">8</a></sup>, and are often used to explain genome evolution in angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d21220e912">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun. 5, 3930 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d21220e915">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d21220e918">8</a></sup>. The genomes of all <i>Brassica</i> species underwent a lineage-specific whole-genome triplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d21220e925">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun. 5, 3930 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d21220e928">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Lysak, M.A., Koch, M.A., Pecinka, A. &amp; Schubert, I. Chromosome triplication found across the tribe Brassiceae. Genome Res. 15, 516â525 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d21220e931">9</a></sup>, followed by diploidization that involved substantial genome reshuffling and gene losses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d21220e935">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bowers, J.E., Chapman, B.A., Rong, J. &amp; Paterson, A.H. Unravelling angiosperm genome evolution by phylogenetic analysis of chromosomal duplication events. Nature 422, 433â438 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d21220e938">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Cheng, F. et al. Deciphering the diploid ancestral genome of the mesohexaploid Brassica rapa. Plant Cell 25, 1541â1554 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d21220e941">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Town, C.D. et al. Comparative genomics of Brassica oleracea and Arabidopsis thaliana reveal gene loss, fragmentation, and dispersal after polyploidy. Plant Cell 18, 1348â1359 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d21220e944">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Mun, J.H. et al. Genome-wide comparative analysis of the Brassica rapa gene space reveals genome shrinkage and differential loss of duplicated genes after whole genome triplication. Genome Biol. 10, R111 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d21220e947">13</a></sup>. In general, plant genomes are typically repetitive, polyploid and heterozygous, which complicates genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Michael, T.P. &amp; VanBuren, R. Progress, challenges and the future of crop genomes. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 24, 71â81 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d21220e952">14</a></sup>. The short read lengths of next-generation sequencing hinder assembly through complex regions, and fragmented draft and reference genomes usually lack skewed (G+C)-content sequences and repetitive intergenic sequences. Furthermore, in allopolyploid species, homoeolog expression dominance or bias, and specifically differential homoelog gene expression, has often been detected, for instance in <i>Gossypium</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Adams, K.L., Cronn, R., Percifield, R. &amp; Wendel, J.F. Genes duplicated by polyploidy show unequal contributions to the transcriptome and organ-specific reciprocal silencing. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 100, 4649â4654 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d21220e958">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Flagel, L., Udall, J., Nettleton, D. &amp; Wendel, J. Duplicate gene expression in allopolyploid Gossypium reveals two temporally distinct phases of expression evolution. BMC Biol. 6, 16 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d21220e961">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d21220e964">17</a></sup>, <i>Triticum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Bottley, A., Xia, G.M. &amp; Koebner, R.M. Homoeologous gene silencing in hexaploid wheat. Plant J. 47, 897â906 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d21220e970">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium (IWGSC). A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d21220e973">19</a></sup> and <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Chang, P.L., Dilkes, B.P., McMahon, M., Comai, L. &amp; Nuzhdin, S.V. Homoeolog-specific retention and use in allotetraploid Arabidopsis suecica depends on parent of origin and network partners. Genome Biol. 11, R125 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d21220e979">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wang, J. et al. Stochastic and epigenetic changes of gene expression in Arabidopsis polyploids. Genetics 167, 1961â1973 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d21220e982">21</a></sup>, but the role of this phenomenon in selection for phenotypic traits remains mechanistically mysterious<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Grover, C.E. et al. Homoeolog expression bias and expression level dominance in allopolyploids. New Phytol. 196, 966â971 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d21220e987">22</a></sup>.</p><p>We reported here the draft genomes of an allopolyploid, <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> var. <i>tumida</i>, constructed by <i>de novo</i> assembly using shotgun reads, single-molecule long reads (PacBio sequencing), genomic (optical) mapping (BioNano sequencing) and genetic mapping, serving to resolve complicated allopolyploid genomes. The multiuse allopolyploid <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> genome offers a distinctive model to study the underlying genomic basis for selection in breeding improvement. These findings place this work into the broader context of plant breeding, highlighting a potential link between homoeolog expression dominance and trait improvement that might be extendable to other polyploid crops.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly, scaffold anchoring and annotation</h3><p>To distinguish among subgenomes in <i>Brassica</i> species, we redesignated the subgenomes in <i>Brassica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Ostergaard, L. &amp; King, G.J. Standardized gene nomenclature for the Brassica genus. Plant Methods 4, 10 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1030">23</a></sup> as follows: <i>B</i>. <i>rapa</i> as BraA; <i>B</i>. <i>nigra</i> as BniB; <i>B</i>. <i>oleracea</i> as BolC; <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> A subgenome as BjuA and B subgenome as BjuB; and <i>B</i>. <i>napus</i> A subgenome as BnaA and C subgenome as BnaC.</p><p>We selected an advanced generation inbred line of <i>B. juncea</i> var. <i>tumida</i> (variety T84â66) for whole-genome sequencing. We estimated the size of the T84-66 genome at 922 Mb by flow cytometry (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 1</a>). We assembled the T84-66 genome using 176Ã Illumina shotgun reads and 12Ã PacBio single-molecule long reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 2a,b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). The assembly spanned 784 Mb, 85% of the 922 Mb estimated by flow cytometry (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 3</a>). The contig N50 value was 61 kb, and the scaffold N50 was 855 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 3</a>).</p><p>We collected 996,648 BioNano DNA molecules over 150 kb, which corresponds to 222 equivalents of the genome, the average of which exceeded 2 Mb in size (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 4</a>). The genome map assembled <i>de novo</i> consisted of 922 constituent genome maps with average length of 1.19 Mb and N50 of 1.84 M (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 4</a>). We used these assemblies to correct the genome assembly above (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). The final assembly by the BioNano approach spanned 955 Mb, and the scaffold N50 was 1.5 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 3</a>). We constructed a high-resolution genetic map with 5,333 bin markers and 18 pseudo-chromosomes (10A and 8B subgenomes; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM77">Supplementary Tables 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">6</a>). We then integrated a published <i>B. juncea</i> genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Zou, J. et al. Co-linearity and divergence of the A subgenome of Brassica juncea compared with other Brassica species carrying different A subgenomes. BMC Genomics 17, 18 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1122">24</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 7</a>). Finally, we anchored 91.5% and 72.3% of A- and B-subgenome assembly sequences onto the 10 and the 8 pseudo-chromosomes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 8a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). We sorted the <i>B. juncea</i> chromosomes into the 402.1 Mb BjuA and 547.5 Mb BjuB subgenomes based on this assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 9</a>).</p><p>We also sequenced the genome of a doubled haploid line of <i>B. nigra</i> (YZ12151) for comparative genomic study. We assembled a collection of 96Ã Illumina shotgun reads to generate a 396.9 Mb genome sequence for <i>B. nigra</i>, with a scaffold N50 of 557.3 kb, and 68% of the estimated 591 Mb <i>B. nigra</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Tables 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">11</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). We anchored the 66% scaffolds into pseudo-chromosomes for <i>B. nigra</i>, referring to the BjuB genetic map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 8b</a>).</p><p>To validate the genome assembly, we used subreads from PacBio, of which 10 subreads had more than 99.4% coverage and 92.3% identity, on average, with the assembled genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 12</a>). We aligned 15 published bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs) from <i>B. nigra</i> to the <i>B. nigra</i> genome assembly, and observed over 98.5% coverage and 99.8% identity on average to BAC clones (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). We BLAST-aligned 458 core eukaryotic genes (Cluster of Essential Genes (CEG) database)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ye, Y.N., Hua, Z.G., Huang, J., Rao, N. &amp; Guo, F.B. CEG: a database of essential gene clusters. BMC Genomics 14, 769 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1190">25</a></sup> to the genome assembly with core eukaryotic genes mapping approach (CEGMA) pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1194">26</a></sup>, which showed high-confidence hits of 453 (98.8%) and 458 (100%) CEG proteins for all 458 essential genes in CEG with full length (&gt;70% alignment) in the genome of B. <i>juncea</i> and <i>B. nigra</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 14a</a>). We validated the assembled genomes by matching expressed sequence tags (ESTs) downloaded from the US National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) database, which indicated that 98.9% and 98.2% ESTs were supported by the assembled genomes of <i>B. juncea</i> and <i>B. nigra</i> (&gt;50% alignments), respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 14b</a>).</p><p>We identified and compared repetitive sequences from syntenic regions of these genomes. We identified 316.1 Mb of repetitive sequence from the <i>B. juncea</i> genome, 131.2 Mb from BjuA and 216.5 Mb from BjuB (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM78">Supplementary Table 15</a>). Long terminal repeats (LTRs) are the predominant transposable element (TE) family identified in all sequenced <i>Brassica</i> genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1229">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun. 5, 3930 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1232">7</a></sup>. <i>Copia-</i> and <i>Gypsy</i>-type LTRs represent the two most abundant TE subfamilies. Using repetitive sequence from syntenic regions, we found that they constituted a similar percentage of all TEs in the BjuA and BjuB, and their respective ancestral genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 7a</a>). We observed similar repetitive sequence contributions in <i>B</i>. <i>napus</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 7b</a>). We identified TEs in the <i>B. juncea</i> and <i>B. napus</i> subgenomes that were newly formed after divergence from each ancestral genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 16a</a>). We confirmed five randomly selected newly formed TEs by PCR amplification from <i>B. rapa</i>, <i>B. nigra</i> and <i>B. juncea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). These newly formed TEs showed similar distribution and percentage between the <i>B. juncea</i> and <i>B. napus</i> subgenomes, and their respective ancestral genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 16b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 10a,b</a>). We observed 310 newly formed TEs to be active between the subgenomes of <i>B. juncea</i>, a much larger number than the 41 newly formed TEs being found active between the subgenomes of <i>B. napus</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 17</a>).</p><p>We annotated 80,050 and 49,826 protein-coding genes in the <i>B. juncea</i> and <i>B. nigra</i> genomes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 18</a>). Approximately 97.8% of <i>B. juncea</i> genes and 94.7% of <i>B. nigra</i> genes could be annotated by non-redundant nucleotide and protein sequences in the NCBI, Cluster of Orthologous Groups (COG), Gene Ontology (GO), SWISS-PROT and Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) databases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 19</a>). Using transcriptomes of <i>B. juncea</i> we validated gene predictions of assembled genomes, verifying all predictions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 20a,b</a>). Additionally, we identified noncoding RNAs, consisting of 21 long noncoding RNAs, 3,725 small RNAs, 2,638 tRNAs, 511 rRNAs, 1,402 microRNAs and 15,418 small nuclear RNAs, from the <i>B. juncea</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 21</a>).</p><p>We extracted 28,228 and 28,917 syntenic ortholog gene pairs from the <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> subgenomes and their ancestral genomes to identify gene loss during the speciation process<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1346">8</a></sup>. In total, we identified 562 and 545 genes lost from BjuA and BjuB, respectively, relative to their common ancestral genomes. This represents a higher percentage than the gene loss estimates for BnaA and BnaC, relative to their common ancestral genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 22</a>). We validated gene loss using PCR amplifications (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). Gene loss numbers of <i>B. juncea</i> and <i>B. napus</i> were consistent with their formation times. The identified genes lost in the <i>B. juncea</i> subgenomes of BjuA and BjuB are involved in different functions based on Gene Ontology (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 12a,b</a>). We mapped the distributions of genes, repetitive sequences, gene loss, pseudogenes, genome markers and genetic markers of the <i>B. juncea</i> subgenomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="The genome of B. juncea vegetable-use variety T84-66."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: The genome of <i>B. juncea</i> vegetable-use variety T84-66.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3657/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3657/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3657_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3657/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3657_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="842"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>The <i>B. juncea</i> genome comprises 10 chromosomes belonging to BjuA (J01âJ10; right semicircle) and 8 chromosomes belonging to BjuB (J11âJ18; left semicircle), scaled on the basis of their assembled length. Homeologous relationships between BjuA and BjuB chromosomes are displayed with connecting lines colored according to the BjuB subgenome. The tracks, from outer to inner, show gene density (non-overlapping, window size = 500 kb), repetitive sequence density (window size = 500 kb), the location of gene loss (blue solid point for gene loss), the location of pseudogenes (solid line for pseudogenes), genome (optical) marker density (window size = 500 kb) and genetic marker density (window size = 500 kb).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3657/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Comparison of A subgenomes in <i>Brassica</i></h3><p>Synteny analysis among three A subgenomes of <i>Brassica</i> showed strong co-linearity, although chromosomal rearrangements have occurred between BjuA and BraA after their divergence from the common <i>B. rapa</i> ancestor (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 23</a>). To study the divergence of BjuA and BnaA, we assayed single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) from the resequencing of A subgenomes from eighteen <i>B. juncea</i> accessions including the <i>B. juncea</i> reference sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 24</a>), five <i>B. napus</i> accessions including the <i>B. napus</i> reference sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1431">8</a></sup>, and 27 <i>B. rapa</i> accessions, including the <i>B. rapa</i> reference sequence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1442">6</a></sup> that covers most subspecies of <i>B. rapa</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 25</a>). We constructed a neighbor-joining tree for A subgenomes in <i>Brassica</i>, and discovered that BjuA and BnaA had divergent origins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). BjuA might derive from <i>B. rapa</i> ssp. <i>tricolaris</i>, which is distributed in Asia, whereas BnaA might derive from <i>B. rapa</i> ssp. <i>rapa</i> (European turnip), which is widely distributed in Europe (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). This discovery indicates that allopolyploids <i>B. juncea</i> and <i>B. napus</i> have independent geographical origins, deriving from Asian and European regions, respectively.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Synteny and phylogenetic evolution analysis of three A-subgenome in Brassica species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Synteny and phylogenetic evolution analysis of three A-subgenome in <i>Brassica</i> species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3657/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3657/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3657_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3657/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3657_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="540"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Schematic representation of synteny among <i>B. rapa</i> (BraA), A subgenome of <i>B. juncea</i> (BjuA) and <i>B. napus</i> (BnaA). Each line connects a pair of orthologous genes between genomes or subgenomes. (<b>b</b>) A phylogenetic neighbor-joining tree constructed from BjuA, BnaA, and resequencing of A subgenomes of 17 <i>B. juncea</i>, 5 <i>B. napus</i> and 27 <i>B. rapa</i> accessions. Vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties of <i>B. juncea</i> are marked with black and rose lines. (<b>c</b>) An ultrametric tree constructed from A subgenomes of <i>B. juncea</i> (BjuA) and <i>B. napus</i> (BnaA); two A subgenomes from the resequencing of <i>B. juncea</i> (BjuA_R16) and <i>B. rapa</i> ssp. <i>tricolaris</i>; and two A subgenomes from the resequencing of <i>B. napus</i> (BnaA) and <i>B. rapa</i> ssp. <i>rapa</i> (Europe). <i>B. oleracea</i> (BolC) is considered as an outgroup. Numbers in parentheses indicate the divergence time interval (Mya). The two A subgenomes from the resequencing of <i>B. juncea</i> (BjuA) and <i>B. rapa</i> (ssp. <i>tricolaris</i>) correspond to the two red and bold branches in <b>b</b>. The two A subgenomes from the resequencing of <i>B. napus</i> (BnaA_R1) and <i>B. rapa</i> ssp. <i>rapa</i> (Europe) correspond to the two blue bold branches in <b>b</b>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3657/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Furthermore, we found that all A subgenomes from <i>B. juncea</i> were rooted in the common ancestor, and evolved into different subvarieties for vegetable or oil use (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). Principal component analysis displayed that vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties of <i>B. juncea</i> were distributed nearby <i>B. rapa</i> ssp. <i>tricolaris</i> group and far from other subspecies of <i>B. rapa</i>, supporting the ancestor being closer to <i>B. rapa</i> ssp. <i>tricolaris</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). Using the independent origin of A subgenomes in <i>B. napus</i> and <i>B. juncea</i> as a control, we compared the SNP variation characteristics between BjuA and BnaA, and that between A subgenomes of vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties of <i>B. juncea</i>. We found typical SNP polyphyletic origin pattern between BjuA and BnaA, and typical SNP monophyletic origin pattern for A subgenomes of vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties in <i>B. juncea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). In total, the results drawn from the phylogenetic tree, principal component analysis and SNP variation patterns point to a monophyletic origin and evolution into vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties for A subgenomes of <i>B. juncea</i>.</p><p>To estimate when <i>B. juncea</i> formed, we found that the synonymous nucleotide substitution rate was not accurate for estimating formation time of the post-neopolyploid species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 26a,b</a>). We therefore used phylogenetic analysis and Bayesian method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Drummond, A.J., Suchard, M.A., Xie, D. &amp; Rambaut, A. Bayesian phylogenetics with BEAUti and the BEAST 1.7. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 1969â1973 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1641">27</a></sup> to calculate when BjuA diverged from its closest relative genome (<i>tricolaris</i>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>), to set an upper limit for its time of formation. We considered the time between the divergence of BjuA and the earliest divergent <i>B. juncea</i> accessions (<i>B. juncea</i>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>) as the lower limit for its formation time. We deduced that <i>B. juncea</i> formed <span class="stix">â¼</span>0.039â0.055 million years ago (Mya) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>). Similarly, to estimate when <i>B. napus</i> formed, we referred to BnaA and its closest relative genome (European <i>rapa</i>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>) to set an upper limit for its formation time, and to BnaA and the earliest divergent <i>B. napus</i> accessions (<i>B. napus</i>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>) to set a lower limit for its formation time. Here we deduced that <i>B. napus</i> formed 0.038â0.051 Mya (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>), which is slightly earlier than the previous estimate of <span class="stix">â¼</span>7,500 years ago derived by synonymous substitution (Ks) estimation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1693">8</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Homoeolog expression dominance in allopolyploid <i>B. juncea</i></h3><p>To explore the transcriptional behavior of the allopolyploid subgenomes, we compared the genome-wide transcriptional levels of homoeologous genes from BjuA and BjuB from different tissues, different developmental stages and two newly resynthesized <i>B. juncea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 27a</a>). On average, 16.2% of genes displayed homoeolog expression dominance in all samples we investigated, of which we observed only 8.2% to be dominantly expressed towards to BjuB over BjuA excluding resynthesized lines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 27b</a>). This indicated no significant global genome dominance using a double-side binomial test for the subgenomes in <i>B. juncea</i>, which may be explained by the recent polyplodization of this crop. This is consistent with several recent polyploids, such as <i>B. napus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1726">8</a></sup>, <i>G. hirsutum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1732">17</a></sup> and <i>T. aestivum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium (IWGSC). A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1738">19</a></sup>. Transcriptional expression analysis in resynthesized <i>Brassica</i> allopolyploids showed that gene expression changes occurred soon after the initial genome merger and allopolyploidization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zhang, D. et al. Genome-specific differential gene expressions in resynthesized Brassica allotetraploids from pair-wise crosses of three cultivated diploids revealed by RNA-seq. Front. Plant Sci. 6, 957 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1746">28</a></sup>. These observations suggest that establishment of homoeolog expression dominance after the initial genome merger and allopolyploidization was immediate. During different developmental stages, 3,339 commonly expressed gene pairs showed homoeolog expression dominance, with 56% of gene pairs displaying dominance toward BjuB subgenomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>). In different tissues, 2,251 commonly expressed gene pairs indicated homoeolog expression dominance, and 55% of gene pairs showed dominance toward BjuB (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>). In all evolutionarily synthesized <i>B. juncea</i>, homoeolog expression dominant genes derived predominantly from BjuB, whereas one of the two transcriptomes from the resynthesized <i>B. juncea</i> types showed expression dominance by BjuA (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 27b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Homoeolog expression dominance and genomic selective pressure analysis in B. juncea."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Homoeolog expression dominance and genomic selective pressure analysis in <i>B. juncea</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3657/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3657/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3657_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3657/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3657_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="825"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Histograms of genome-wide expression of syntenic homoeologous genes among indicated <i>B. juncea</i> tissues and developmental stages. <i>N</i> values indicate the number of dominant genes in BjuA and BjuB, respectively. (<b>b</b>) Boxplot of the distribution of Ka/Ks values between subgenomes (BjuA and BjuB) and among homoeolog expression dominance genes as dominant, subordinate and neutral (non-dominance) in <i>B. juncea</i>. In the left boxplot, Ka/Ks values range from 0.139 to 0.393 with median value 0.238 and interquartile range (IQR) value 0.209 for BjuA, and Ka/Ks values range from 0.147 to 0.411 with median value 0.260 and IQR value 0.224 for BjuB. In the right boxplot, Ka/Ks values range from 0.106 to 0.334 with median value 0.189 and IQR value 0.164 for dominant group, and Ka/Ks values range from 0.193 to 0.438 with median value 0.328 and IQR value 0.279 for subordinate group, and Ka/Ks values range from 0.082 to 0.260 with median value 0.139 and IQR value 0.123 for neutral group. *<i>P</i> &lt; 0.001, permutation test with 1,000 permutations.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3657/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We identified 5,632 gene pairs displaying homoeolog expression dominance in <i>B. juncea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM79">Supplementary Table 28</a>). Using the KEGG database, we performed a pathway enrichment analysis for all homoeolog expression dominant genes. This analysis showed that genes showing homoeolog expression dominance were enriched for: cellular processes, environmental information processing, genetic information processing and metabolism and plant-pathogen interaction (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>). Among these pathways, we found that metabolic and plant hormone signal transduction pathways were especially enriched.</p><p>We calculated the average non-synonymous/synonymous substitution (Ka/Ks) value of all genes among population accessions based on whole-genome sequencing and resequencing data. The results show significant difference between BjuA and BjuB using a permutation test, of which the BjuB has evolved faster than the BjuA, suggesting asymmetric evolution of the two subgenomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). To further analyze homoeolog expression dominant genes, we calculated the average Ka/Ks values among those genes expressed as dominant (higher expression level in homoeologous gene pair), subordinate (lower expression level in homoeologous gene pair) and neutral (equal expression level in homoeologous gene pair). The Ka/Ks values of dominant and subordinate genes (median: 0.31 and 0.35, respectively) were significantly higher than those of neutral genes (median: 0.25) using a permutation test (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). We also calculated the average Ka value among these genes, which indicated the same patterns with Ka/Ks values (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>). This observation indicated that both dominant and subordinate genes evolved more rapidly than did the neutral genes, with subordinate genes being prone to selection in a homoelogous gene pair.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Selection in allopolyploid <i>B. juncea</i></h3><p>Using SNPs from resequencing accessions of <i>B. juncea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 25</a>), we estimated the average pairwise diversity (Ï) and population differentiation statistics (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) between the vegetable- and oil-use varieties of <i>B. juncea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM80">Supplementary Tables 29</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM81">30</a>). We identified selective sweep regions in vegetable- and oil-use <i>B. juncea</i> accessions by combining <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> &lt; 0.05 and Ï ratio &lt; 0.05 outliers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 31</a>). In total, we identified 794 selected genes between the vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties of <i>B. juncea</i>, of which 36.3% (288) showed homoeolog expression dominance. A high proportion of genes with homoeolog expression dominance under selection imply their participation in agricultural trait improvement.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Selective sweep signals and expression pattern analysis between vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties of B. juncea."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Selective sweep signals and expression pattern analysis between vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties of <i>B. juncea</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3657/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3657/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3657_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3657/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3657_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="955"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Genome-wide distribution of <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values and Ï ratio (sliding window = 100 kb, step = 10 kb). Red dashed lines represent the 95% tails for the empirical <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> distribution (top) and Ï ratio (bottom). Magnified are regions of two GSL-metabolism-related genes (<i>BjuA020134</i> and <i>BjuA020135</i>) and two lipid-metabolism-related genes (<i>BjuA020878</i> and <i>BjuA030837</i>) that showed both homoeolog expression dominance and strong selective signals, respectively. (<b>b</b>) Sequence analysis of selected genes in <b>a</b> between vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties. The non-synonymous mutation sites are shown on white background. (<b>c</b>) Heat map for genes involved in GSL and lipid synthesis in vegetable-use (highlighted in green) and oil-use (highlighted in orange) subvarieties of <i>B. juncea</i> from the RNA-seq data. Scaled log<sub>2</sub> expression values are shown from red to blue in color, indicating high (red) to low (blue) expression. Abbreviations of sample names in <b>c</b> are defined in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM83">Supplementary Table 33</a>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3657/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Vegetable-use <i>B. juncea</i> varieties have been selected based on their glucosinolate (GSL) content and composition for human nutrition and plant defense properties<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Verkerk, R. et al. Glucosinolates in Brassica vegetables: the influence of the food supply chain on intake, bioavailability and human health. Mol. Nutr. Food Res. 53 (Suppl. 2), S219âS265 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1937">29</a></sup>. Oil-use <i>B. juncea</i> varieties have been subjected to intensive breeding to improve their lipid composition, including decreases in the levels of erucic acid and GSL, which are undesirable because they can produce toxic catabolic products in animal feed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1944">8</a></sup>. In total, we identified 13 GSL-metabolism-related genes and 22 lipid-metabolism-related genes that were differentially selected between the vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties of <i>B. juncea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM82">Supplementary Table 32</a>). Of these selected genes, 6 GSL-metabolism-related genes and 7 lipid-metabolism-related genes likewise exhibited homoeolog expression dominance (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 20</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM83">Supplementary Table 33</a>). One of these genes, <i>BjuB021254</i>, whose ortholog is <i>AT1G04350</i> (<i>AOP</i>) in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, encodes a 2-oxoglutarate-dependent dioxygenase and has an essential role in GSL biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="SÃ¸nderby, I.E., Geu-Flores, F. &amp; Halkier, B.A. Biosynthesis of glucosinolates-gene discovery and beyond. Trends Plant Sci. 15, 283â290 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1974">30</a></sup>. The gene <i>BjuA030837</i>, whose ortholog is <i>AT1G18460</i> in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, encodes a lipase family protein that is important in the glycerol biosynthesis process<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Li-Beisson, Y. et al. Acly-lipid metabolism. in The Arabidopsis Book. The American Society of Plant Biologists 8, e0133 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d21220e1987">31</a></sup>. The homoeolog expression dominance and selection of these genes suggests that their functions in GSL and lipid metabolism have been subjected to selection and improvement between vegetable and oil-use subvarieties of <i>B. juncea</i>.</p><p>With resequencing of vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties of <i>B. juncea</i>, we showed divergent genotypes with nonsynonymous mutation for GSL- and lipid-metabolism-related genes between vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties <i>of B. juncea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). Oil-use types displayed uniform genotypes for the GSL- and lipid-metabolism-related genes compared to vegetable-use types. In the vegetable-use groups, all but varieties CN59 and CN79 showed consistent genotypes for these genes. We constructed a phylogenetic tree for the vegetable- and oil-use types of <i>B. juncea</i>, in which accessions CN59 and CN79 were clustered into a subgroup independent from the vegetable-use subgroup (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>), although we classified them into the vegetable-use subgroup on the basis of their edible organs. Transcriptomic analysis from selected GSL- and lipid-metabolism-associated genes showed significant differences in expression using a two-tailed <i>t</i>-test between vegetable and oil-use subvarieties of <i>B. juncea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM80">Supplementary Table 29</a>). These observations indicate that genomic selection has diversified GSL- and lipid-metabolism-related genes between vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties of the plant, each in the direction of their respective agriculturally desirable traits. We also observed 24 selected genes involved in phytohormone metabolism, of which 12 genes exhibited homoeolog expression dominance (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM82">Supplementary Table 32</a>). Transcriptomic analysis for selected phytohormone-associated genes showed significant differences in expression using a two tailed t-test between vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties of <i>B. juncea</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM83">Supplementary Table 33</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 21</a>). These differences may, likewise, contribute to the phenotypic deviations between the two types.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p><i>B. rapa</i>, <i>B. juncea</i> and <i>B. napus</i> once comprised the three main <i>Brassica</i> oilseed crops worldwide, whereas at present <i>B. rapa</i> is selected primarily as a vegetable, <i>B. juncea</i> both as a vegetable and for oil use, and <i>B. napus</i> for oilseed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Schmidt, R. &amp; Bancroft, I. Brassicaceae in agriculture. in Genetics and Genomics of the Brassicaceae 33â65 (Springer, 2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2069">32</a></sup>. Discovery of a possible A-subgenome-diversified origin for <i>B. juncea</i> and <i>B. napus</i> may shed light on the unusual features of selection divergence in <i>Brassica</i>. These insights appear promising for <i>de novo</i> synthesis of neo-polyploid species by introgression of individual A-subgenome types to achieve desired breeding purposes.</p><p>We demonstrated evidence of homoeolog expression dominance patterns distinguishing A-subgenome types in <i>Brassica</i>. Although homoeolog expression dominance has been observed in several polyploid species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2091">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium (IWGSC). A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2094">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Grover, C.E. et al. Homoeolog expression bias and expression level dominance in allopolyploids. New Phytol. 196, 966â971 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2097">22</a></sup>, a correlation between homoeolog expression dominance and evolutionary rate has not been reported previously. This finding provides important evidence of agricultural selection behavior. More importantly, these observations may facilitate the improvement of agriculturally important traits by focusing selection on the transcriptionally dominant genes.</p><p>Selective sweep regions distinguishing vegetable- and oil-use subvarieties of <i>B. juncea</i> identified 794 loci, of which 36.3% showed homoeolog expression dominance. This high proportion of genes with expression dominance under selection implies their potential in agricultural trait improvement by precisely associating homoeolog expression dominance genes with target traits (36.3% selected genes of 16.2% homoeolog expression dominance genes). It is reasonable to assume that this methodology could be applied to a broader array of selected traits in other polyploid crops to provide insight into underlying physiological mechanisms.</p><p>Polyploidy is particularly common in flowering plants, is recognized as a characteristic of all angiosperm genomes during their evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97â100 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2110">33</a></sup> and has an essential role in speciation and genomic plasticity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Leitch, A.R. &amp; Leitch, I.J. Genomic plasticity and the diversity of polyploid plants. Science 320, 481â483 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2114">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Soltis, P.S. &amp; Soltis, D.E. The role of hybridization in plant speciation. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 60, 561â588 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2117">35</a></sup>. The reprogramming of allopolyploid transcriptomes is shown to be triggered predominantly by interspecific hybridization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Chang, P.L., Dilkes, B.P., McMahon, M., Comai, L. &amp; Nuzhdin, S.V. Homoeolog-specific retention and use in allotetraploid Arabidopsis suecica depends on parent of origin and network partners. Genome Biol. 11, R125 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2121">20</a></sup>, displaying insight into homoeolog gene expression in phenotypic variability and plasticity. Homoeolog expression dominance or bias appears to be a consequence of genome merger and doubling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Grover, C.E. et al. Homoeolog expression bias and expression level dominance in allopolyploids. New Phytol. 196, 966â971 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2125">22</a></sup>, but the underlying applicability of the homoeolog expression dominance in agriculture trait selection has not been substantiated to date. We found that homoeolog expression dominant genes have higher Ka/Ks than neutral genes in the allopolyploid <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i>, consistent with these genes as targets of intensified selection for vegetable- and oil-use varieties of this agriculturally important plant. This observation implies that transcriptional dominance can predate trait selection. The potential linking of homoeolog expression dominance to trait improvement suggests that <i>Brassica</i> breeding programs, and those of other polyploid crops, might benefit from focusing their efforts on the subset of genes with transcriptional dominance, both as a means of enhancing response to selection and toward gaining mechanistic insights.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Plant materials and sequencing.</h3><p>Genome sequencing and assembly was done on a <i>B</i>. <i>juncea var. tumida</i> inbred line (T84â66) with excellent agronomic traits being widely used as a parent in breeding (NCBI Biosample SAMN03741772) and a <i>B. nigra</i> double haploid line (YZ12151) (NCBI Biosample SAMN03742614). Sequences of T84â66 included 13 paired-end and mate-paired Illumina libraries (175.8Ã) and 1 single-molecule reads library (12.03Ã) combined with 222Ã of BioNano data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Tables 2a,b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">4</a>). Sequences of YZ12151 included 10 paired-end and mate-paired Illumina libraries (95.99Ã) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 10</a>). The flow cytometry analysis and the abundance of 17-nt <i>k</i>-mers were performed to estimate the genome size (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 1</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Figs. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">5</a>). Additionally, about 10Ã coverage of genome sequences from 17 <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> varieties consisting of 10 vegetable- and 7 oil-use subvarieties for each were generated for genomic analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 24</a>). Low-depth (&lt;1Ã) genome sequencing of 27 representative <i>B. rapa</i> accessions were used for comparative analysis of A subgenomes in <i>Brassica</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10"><i>De novo</i> assembly.</h3><p>Genome assembly used ALLPATHS-LG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Maccallum, I. et al. ALLPATHS 2: small genomes assembled accurately and with high continuity from short paired reads. Genome Biol. 10, R103 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2212">36</a></sup>. All the corrected Pacbio RS II reads were used to fill the gaps by PBjelly_V15.2.20 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="English, A.C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS One 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2215">37</a>). RefAligner utility in IrysView was used to perform alignment between Irys molecules and draft assemblies for correcting the scaffolds chimera error. Finally, the corrected scaffolds were anchored to the genomic (optical) maps assembled from BioNano data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). This generated assembly v1.0 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 3</a>). Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome quality evaluation.</h3><p>We used the CEGMA v.2.3 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ye, Y.N., Hua, Z.G., Huang, J., Rao, N. &amp; Guo, F.B. CEG: a database of essential gene clusters. BMC Genomics 14, 769 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2235">25</a>) to blast 458 conserved Core eukaryotic genes (CGE database)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Zou, J. et al. Co-linearity and divergence of the A subgenome of Brassica juncea compared with other Brassica species carrying different A subgenomes.
