BMC Genomics 17, 18 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2239">24</a></sup> to assess the genome assembly of <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i>. The assembled genome of <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> was also validated by mapping 23,002 ESTs (length â¥ 500 bp) downloaded from NCBI. To assess the accuracy of the <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> genome, we randomly aligned 10 sub-reads over 40 kb from PacBio data to check the paired end relationship using SOAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Gu, S., Fang, L. &amp; Xu, X. Using SOAPaligner for short reads alignment. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics 44, 1â17 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2262">38</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Tables 12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">14a,b</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genetic map and pseudo-chromosome construction.</h3><p>We constructed a reference genetic map of <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> based on genotyping by resequencing of 100 individuals of F<sub>2</sub> population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Huang, X. et al. High-throughput genotyping by whole-genome resequencing. Genome Res. 19, 1068â1076 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2295">39</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM77">Supplementary Table 5</a>). After resequencing reads alignment with BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with BurrowsâWheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2303">40</a></sup>, potential SNPs were identified by GATK v3.4 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="DePristo, M.A. et al. A framework for variation discovery and genotyping using next-generation DNA sequencing data. Nat. Genet. 43, 491â498 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2306">41</a>). Pairwise recombination of this marker set on each scaffold was calculated, of which adjacent SNPs with pairwise recombination rate less than 0.001 were lumped into a genetic bin, excluding bins showing significantly distorted segregation (chi-squared test, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.01). A final set of bin markers was grouped to 18 linkage groups using Highmap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Liu, D. et al. Construction and analysis of high-density linkage map using high-throughput sequencing data. PLoS One 9, e98855 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2313">42</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 8a</a>).</p><p>ALLMAPS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Tang, H. et al. ALLMAPS: robust scaffold ordering based on multiple maps. Genome Biol. 16, 3 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2323">43</a></sup> was used to construct the initial pseudo-chromosomes of <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> from scaffolds using the genetic map (T84/DTC) constructed in the present study being integrated with a published genetic map (SY/PM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Ostergaard, L. &amp; King, G.J. Standardized gene nomenclature for the Brassica genus. Plant Methods 4, 10 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2333">23</a></sup>. We sorted BjuA and BjuB subgenomes of <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> referred to the final genetic map (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 9</a>). Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome annotation.</h3><p>The repetitive sequences of the <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> genome were identified with a combination of <i>de novo</i> and homolog strategies. Four <i>de novo</i> programs including RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Price, A.L., Jones, N.C. &amp; Pevzner, P.A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21 (Suppl. 1), i351âi358 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2371">44</a></sup>, LTR-FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2376">45</a></sup>, MITE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Han, Y. &amp; Wessler, S.R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e199 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2380">46</a></sup> and PILER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Edgar, R.C. &amp; Myers, E.W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21 (Suppl. 1), i152âi158 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2384">47</a></sup> were used to generate the initial repeat library. The initial repeat database was classified into classes, subclasses, superfamilies and families by the PASTEClassifier with REPET<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Wicker, T., Matthews, D.E. &amp; Keller, B. TREP: a database for Triticeae repetitive elements. Trends Plant Sci. 7, 561â562 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2388">48</a></sup>. We then merged transposable element (TE) sequences of <i>Brassica</i> species and the Repbase database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Bao, W., Kojima, K.K. &amp; Kohany, O. Repbase Update, a database of repetitive elements in eukaryotic genomes. Mob. DNA 6, 11 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2395">49</a></sup> together to construct a new repeat database and distinguish the genome assembly repeat sequences through RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics Chapter 4, Unit 4.10 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2400">50</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM78">Supplementary Table 15</a>).</p><p>Genes were annotated iteratively using three main approaches: homology-based, <i>de novo</i> and EST/unigenes-based. Results of these three methods were integrated by GLEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Elsik, C.G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2413">51</a></sup> to get a high-confidence gene model. An RNA-seq based method mapping transcriptome data to the reference genome using TopHat and assembling transcripts with Cufflinks was adopted to obtain the gene structures and new genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562â578 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2417">52</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Tables 18, 19</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">20a,b</a>).</p><p>tRNAscan-SEM (version 1.23)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Lowe, T.M. &amp; Eddy, S.R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955â964 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2430">53</a></sup> was used to detect reliable tRNA positions. Noncoding RNAs were predicted by the Infernal program using default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Nawrocki, E.P. &amp; Eddy, S.R. Infernal 1.1: 100-fold faster RNA homology searches. Bioinformatics 29, 2933â2935 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2434">54</a></sup>. Through comparing the similarity of secondary structure between the <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> sequence and Rfam (v12.0) database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Nawrocki, E.P. et al. Rfam 12.0: updates to the RNA families database. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D130âD137 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2444">55</a></sup>, the noncoding RNAs were classified into different families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 21</a>).</p><p>Stringent criteria and strategy were used to identify new TEs for the BjuA subgenome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). The same strategy was used to identify new TEs in the subgenomes of <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> and <i>B</i>. <i>napus</i> compared to their corresponding ancestral genome after divergence from a common ancestor (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 16a,b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>We performed all-against-all BLASTP (<i>E</i> = 1 Ã 10<sup>â5</sup>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="KieÅbasa, S.M., Wan, R., Sato, K., Horton, P. &amp; Frith, M.C. Adaptive seeds tame genomic sequence comparison. Genome Res. 21, 487â493 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2486">56</a></sup> and chained the BLASTP hits by QUOTA-ALIGN (cscore = 0.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Tang, H. et al. Screening synteny blocks in pairwise genome comparisons through integer programming. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 102 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2490">57</a></sup> with '1:1 synteny screen' to call synteny blocks. The '1:3 synteny screen' model was used to identify synteny blocks between <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>Brassica</i> because of whole genome triplication in <i>Brassica</i> evolution history<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wang, X. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035â1039 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2504">6</a></sup> by QUOTA-ALIGN (cscore = 0.5). All gene losses were calculated based on the <i>Brassica</i> ancestor common gene sets of each species. Meanwhile, we identified gene loss from other subgenomes (BniB, BjuB, BolC, BnaA and BnaC) of <i>Brassica</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Tables 22</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">34</a>). Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Comparison of A subgenomes in <i>Brassica</i>.</h3><p>We called SNPs from A subgenomes by resequencing of <i>B. juncea</i>, <i>B. napus</i> and <i>B. rapa</i> and referring to the <i>B. rapa</i> reference genome using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with BurrowsâWheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754â1760 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2548">40</a></sup>, GATK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="DePristo, M.A. et al. A framework for variation discovery and genotyping using next-generation DNA sequencing data. Nat. Genet. 43, 491â498 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2553">41</a></sup> and SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2557">58</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 25</a>). Ungenotyped SNPs were imputed by the KNN algorithms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Chen, W. et al. Genome-wide association analyses provide genetic and biochemical insights into natural variation in rice metabolism. Nat. Genet. 46, 714â721 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2564">59</a></sup>. SNPs with minor allele frequency (MAF) &gt; 0.05 were picked for further analysis. Only non-heterozygous SNPs with integrity &gt; 0.6 were kept for phylogenetic tree construction. The neighbor-joining phylogenetic tree for A subgenomes in <i>Brassica</i> was constructed by MEGA v6.0 using the Kimura 2-parameter model with 1,000 bootstraps and default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Tamura, K., Stecher, G., Peterson, D., Filipski, A. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA6: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis version 6.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 2725â2729 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2571">60</a></sup>.</p><p>We selected high quality SNPs with integrity â¥ 0.8 and MAF â¥ 0.05 from all SNPs above for principal component analysis using STRATPCA program from the EIGENSOFT package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Price, A.L. et al. Principal components analysis corrects for stratification in genome-wide association studies. Nat. Genet. 38, 904â909 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2578">61</a></sup>.</p><p>To compare the characteristics of the SNPs of <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> and <i>B</i>. <i>napus</i>, we selected six <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> varieties including three vegetable- and three oil-use subvarieties (CN53, CN58, CN04 and CN02, EU07, AU213, respectively) and five <i>B</i>. <i>napus</i> varieties. We only retained SNPs with full integrity (integrity = 1) for further analysis. Fixed SNPs were defined as the frequency of alleles â¥ 60% and were different from their reference genome in <i>B. juncea</i> and <i>B. napus</i> populations. Polymorphic SNPs in <i>B. juncea</i> population were defined as the frequency of alleles â¥ 60%, and their genotypings were distinct from <i>B. napus</i>. Polymorphic SNPs in <i>B. napus</i> population were defined as the frequency of alleles â¥ 60% and their genotypings were dissimilar to <i>B. juncea</i>. We identified fixed and polymorphic SNPs in <i>B. juncea</i> and <i>B. napus</i> populations and those between <i>B. juncea</i> and <i>B. napus</i> population based on different frequencies of alleles scaled at 60%, 70%, 80% and 90%. We used the same strategies to identify fixed and polymorphic SNPs in and between vegetable- and oil-use <i>B. juncea</i>. Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Formation time estimation for <i>B. juncea</i>.</h3><p>To estimate the formation time of <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i>, we first selected BjuA, its closest relative genome from <i>B. rapa</i> and the earliest divergent <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> accession based on the phylogenetic tree of A subgenomes in <i>Brassica</i>. Then we reconstructed the coding region sequences for selected varieties from the resequencing data. After multiple sequence alignments by MUSCLE v3.3 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Edgar, R.C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2678">62</a>), a phylogenetic tree was constructed and divergence time was estimated by Bayesian MCMC analyses in BEASTv1.8 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Drummond, A.J., Suchard, M.A., Xie, D. &amp; Rambaut, A. Bayesian phylogenetics with BEAUti and the BEAST 1.7. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 1969â1973 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2681">27</a>) with JIT nucleotide substitution model, relaxed log normal clock model, and one million MCMC generations from which parameters were sampled every 1,000 generations and other default parameters. The divergence time of <i>B</i>. <i>oleracea</i> (4.6 Â± 0.5 Mya) was considered as outgroup<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun. 5, 3930 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2691">7</a></sup>. We calculated the divergence time between BjuA and its closest relative genome from <i>B. rapa</i> as the upper limit of formation time of <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i>. The divergence time between BjuA and the earliest divergent <i>B. juncea</i> accessions was considered as the lower limit of formation time of <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i>. Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Homoeolog expression dominance analysis.</h3><p>The clean reads from RNA-seq after quality control were mapped onto the <i>B</i>. <i>juncea</i> genome using Tophat2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2731">63</a>). The gene expression level of individual genes was quantified using RPKM values (fragments per kilobase of exon per million fragments mapped) by Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562â578 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2735">52</a></sup>. Homoeolog expression dominance analysis was performed within syntenic gene pairs. Differentially expressed genes pairs with greater than twofold change were defined as dominant gene pairs. The dominant genes were the genes that were expressed relatively higher in dominant gene pairs, and the lower ones are the subordinate genes. The rest of the syntenic gene pairs that showed non-dominance were classified as neutral genes. To test whether the occurrences of BjuA dominant gene pairs and the occurrences of BjuB dominant gene pairs are equal, we performed double-side binomial tests on dominant gene pairs for all samples<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Schnable, J.C., Springer, N.M. &amp; Freeling, M. Differentiation of the maize subgenomes by genome dominance and both ancient and ongoing gene loss. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 4069â4074 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2739">64</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 27a,b</a>). Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Selective pressure on dominantly expressed genes and subgenomes.</h3><p>All SNP sets were called by GATK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="DePristo, M.A. et al. A framework for variation discovery and genotyping using next-generation DNA sequencing data. Nat. Genet. 43, 491â498 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2758">41</a></sup> for 17 <i>B. juncea</i> accessions with default parameters and filtered out with depth &lt; 3Ã. Coding region sequence sets were then reconstructed based on high quality SNPs for each sample. To detect selective pressure of each coding gene, the rates of nonsynonymous (dN) and synonymous (dS) (Ï = dN/dS) substitutions were estimated site-by-site using the YN00 program with default parameters from the PAML 4.2b package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2765">58</a></sup>. Each paired gene set of 17 samples was estimated repeatedly. All Ka/Ks of gene pairs were classified to three categories (dominant genes, subordinate genes and neutral genes). Meanwhile All Ka/Ks of gene pairs were separated into BjuA/BjuB subgenomes. To test statistical significance of different data sets, we performed a permutation test on them with 1,000 permutations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 26a,b</a>). Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Detection of selective sweep signals.</h3><p>Average pairwise diversity (Ï) and population differentiation statistic (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) were calculated through Bio::PopGen of bioperl package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Stajich, J.E. et al. The Bioperl toolkit: Perl modules for the life sciences. Genome Res. 12, 1611â1618 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3657#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d21220e2787">65</a></sup>. Selective sweep regions were identified in the 10 vegetable- and 7 oil-use <i>B. juncea</i> subvarieties by combining <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> outliers and Ï ratio outliers (Î¸Ï (vegetable-use/oil-use)) with 100 kb sliding windows and 10 kb steps. Adjacent windows extended to 10 kb likely represent the effect of a single divergence region and thus were linked to define a 'candidate gene region' (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Table 31</a>). Additional details are available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3657#MOESM76">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">URLs.</h3><p>PBjelly, <a href="https://sourceforge.net/projects/pb-jelly/">https://sourceforge.net/projects/pb-jelly/</a>; <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>B. rapa</i> protein sequences, <a href="http://genome.jgi-psf.org/">http://genome.jgi-psf.org/</a>; PASA, <a href="http://pasapipeline.github.io/">http://pasapipeline.github.io/</a>; KEGG Automatic Annotation Server; <a href="http://www.genome.jp/kegg/kaas/">http://www.genome.jp/kegg/kaas/</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Accession codes.</h3><p>The genome assemblies of <i>B. juncea</i> and <i>B. nigra</i> have been deposited at GenBank: <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=LFQT00000000">LFQT00000000</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=LFLV00000000">LFLV00000000</a>, respectively (BioProject <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=PRJNA285130">PRJNA285130</a>).</p></div></div></section>
